	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Mother's Day</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Mother%27s_Day_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Mother&#39;s Day (disambiguation)">Mother's Day (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">celebration honouring mothers</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="infobox vevent" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="hd" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background-color: transparent">Mother's Day</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Observed&#160;by</th><td>40+ countries</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Type</th><td>Worldwide</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Significance</th><td class="summary">Honors mothers and motherhood</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Date</th><td>Varies per country</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Frequency</th><td>Annual</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Related&#160;to</th><td><a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Day" title="Children&#39;s Day">Children's Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siblings_Day" title="Siblings Day">Siblings Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/Father%27s_Day" title="Father&#39;s Day">Father's Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parents%27_Day" title="Parents&#39; Day">Parents' Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Grandparents_Day" title="National Grandparents Day">Grandparent's Day</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Mother's Day</b> is a celebration honoring the <a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">mother</a> of the family, as well as <a href="/wiki/Motherhood" class="mw-redirect" title="Motherhood">motherhood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maternal_bond" title="Maternal bond">maternal bonds</a>, and the influence of mothers in society. It is celebrated on various days in many parts of the world, most commonly in the months of March or May. It complements similar celebrations honoring family members, such as <a href="/wiki/Father%27s_Day" title="Father&#39;s Day">Father's Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siblings_Day" title="Siblings Day">Siblings Day</a>, and <a href="/wiki/National_Grandparents_Day" title="National Grandparents Day">Grandparents Day</a>.
</p><p>The modern Mother's Day began in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, at the initiative of <a href="/wiki/Anna_Jarvis" title="Anna Jarvis">Anna Jarvis</a> in the early 20th century. This is not (directly) related to the many traditional celebrations of mothers and motherhood that have existed throughout the world over thousands of years, such as the Greek cult to <a href="/wiki/Cybele" title="Cybele">Cybele</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(mythology)" title="Rhea (mythology)">Rhea</a> the Great Mother of the Gods, the Roman festival of <a href="/wiki/Hilaria" title="Hilaria">Hilaria</a>, or the Christian <a href="/wiki/Mothering_Sunday" title="Mothering Sunday">Mothering Sunday</a> celebration (originally a commemoration of Mother Church, not motherhood).<sup id="cite_ref-grold_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grold-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tuleja_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tuleja-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-myers_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-myers-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> However, in some countries, Mother's Day is still synonymous with these older traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The U.S.-derived modern version of Mother's Day has been criticized<sup id="cite_ref-why_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-why-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> for having become too commercialized. Founder Jarvis herself regretted this <a href="/wiki/Commercialism" title="Commercialism">commercialism</a> and expressed views on how that was never her intention.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_of_holiday">Establishment of holiday</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mother%27s_Day_(United_States)#History" title="Mother&#39;s Day (United States)">Mother's Day (United States) §&#160;History</a></div>
<p>The modern holiday of Mother's Day was first celebrated in 1908, when <a href="/wiki/Anna_Jarvis" title="Anna Jarvis">Anna Jarvis</a> held a memorial for her mother at St Andrew's Methodist Church in <a href="/wiki/Grafton,_West_Virginia" title="Grafton, West Virginia">Grafton, West Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> St Andrew's Methodist Church now holds the <a href="/wiki/International_Mother%27s_Day_Shrine" title="International Mother&#39;s Day Shrine">International Mother's Day Shrine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Reilly2010_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Reilly2010-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Her campaign to make Mother's Day a recognized holiday in the United States began in 1905, the year her mother, <a href="/wiki/Ann_Jarvis" title="Ann Jarvis">Ann Reeves Jarvis</a>, died. Ann Jarvis had been a peace activist who cared for wounded soldiers on both sides of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, and created Mother's Day Work Clubs to address public health issues. Anna Jarvis wanted to honor her mother by continuing the work she started and to set aside a day to honor all mothers because she believed a mother is "the person who has done more for you than anyone in the world".<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1908, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Congress">U.S. Congress</a> rejected a proposal to make Mother's Day an official holiday, joking that they would also have to proclaim a "Mother-in-law's Day".<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> However, owing to the efforts of Anna Jarvis, by 1911 all U.S. states observed the holiday,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> with some of them officially recognizing Mother's Day as a local holiday<sup id="cite_ref-RiceTedesco2015_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RiceTedesco2015-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> (the first being West Virginia, Jarvis' home state, in 1910). In 1914, Woodrow Wilson signed a proclamation designating Mother's Day, held on the second Sunday in May, as a national holiday to honor mothers.<sup id="cite_ref-deseret_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deseret-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Jarvis was successful in founding Mother's Day, she became resentful of the <a href="/wiki/Hallmark_holiday" title="Hallmark holiday">commercialization of the holiday</a>. By the early 1920s, <a href="/wiki/Hallmark_Cards" title="Hallmark Cards">Hallmark Cards</a> and other companies had started selling Mother's Day cards. Jarvis believed that the companies had misinterpreted and exploited the idea of Mother's Day, and that the emphasis of the holiday was on sentiment, not profit. As a result, she organized boycotts of Mother's Day, and threatened to issue <a href="/wiki/Lawsuits" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawsuits">lawsuits</a> against the companies involved.<sup id="cite_ref-kansas2014_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kansas2014-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Jarvis argued that people should appreciate and honor their mothers through handwritten letters expressing their love and gratitude, instead of buying gifts and pre-made cards.<sup id="cite_ref-deseret_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deseret-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Jarvis protested at a candy makers' convention in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> in 1923, and at a meeting of <a href="/wiki/American_War_Mothers" title="American War Mothers">American War Mothers</a> in 1925. By this time, <a href="/wiki/Carnation" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnation">carnations</a> had become associated with Mother's Day, and the selling of carnations by the American War Mothers to raise money angered Jarvis, who was arrested for <a href="/wiki/Breach_of_the_peace" title="Breach of the peace">disturbing the peace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-deseret_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deseret-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kansas2014_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kansas2014-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling">Spelling</span></h2>
<p>In 1912 Anna Jarvis trademarked the phrase "Second Sunday in May, Mother's Day, Anna Jarvis, Founder", and created the Mother's Day International Association.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> She specifically noted that "Mother's" should "be a singular possessive, for each family to honor its own mother, not a plural possessive commemorating all mothers in the world."<sup id="cite_ref-vancouversun_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vancouversun-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> This is also the spelling used by U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> in his 1914 presidential proclamation, by the U.S. Congress in relevant bills,<sup id="cite_ref-vote274_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vote274-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vote275_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vote275-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and by various U.S. presidents in their proclamations concerning Mother's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dates_around_the_world">Dates around the world</span></h2>
<p>While the United States holiday was adopted by some other countries, existing celebrations, held on different dates, honoring motherhood have become described as "Mother's Day", such as <a href="/wiki/Mothering_Sunday" title="Mothering Sunday">Mothering Sunday</a> in the United Kingdom<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> or, in Greece, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> celebration of the presentation of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> to the temple (2 February of Julian Calendar). Both the secular and religious Mother Day are present in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Mothering Sunday is often referred to as "Mother's Day" even though it is an unrelated celebration.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In some countries, the date adopted is one significant to the majority religion, such as <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> Day in Catholic countries. Other countries selected a date with historical significance. For example, Bolivia's Mother's Day is a fixed date, remembering of a battle in which women participated to defend their children.<sup id="cite_ref-bolivia_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bolivia-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> See the "<a href="#International_history_and_tradition">International history and tradition</a>" section for the complete list.
</p><p>Some ex-socialist countries, such as <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, celebrated <a href="/wiki/International_Women%27s_Day" title="International Women&#39;s Day">International Women's Day</a> instead of Mother's Day<sup id="cite_ref-saunders_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-saunders-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> or simply celebrate both holidays, which is the custom in Ukraine.  Kyrgyzstan has recently introduced Mother's Day, but "year on year <a href="/wiki/International_Women%27s_Day" title="International Women&#39;s Day">International Women's Day</a> is certainly increasing in status".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mother%27s_Day.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Mother%27s_Day.jpg/220px-Mother%27s_Day.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Mother%27s_Day.jpg/330px-Mother%27s_Day.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Mother%27s_Day.jpg/440px-Mother%27s_Day.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3296" data-file-height="2472" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mother%27s_Day.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mother's Day gift in 2007</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mother%27s_day_card.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Mother%27s_day_card.jpg/220px-Mother%27s_day_card.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Mother%27s_day_card.jpg/330px-Mother%27s_day_card.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Mother%27s_day_card.jpg/440px-Mother%27s_day_card.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1279" data-file-height="851" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mother%27s_day_card.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mother and daughter and Mother's Day card</div></div></div>
<p>In most countries, Mother's Day is an observance derived from the holiday as it has evolved in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, promoted by companies who saw benefit in making it popular.<sup id="cite_ref-why_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-why-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> As adopted by other countries and cultures, the holiday has different meanings, is associated with different events (religious, historical or legendary), and is celebrated on different dates.
</p><p>In some cases, countries already had existing celebrations honoring motherhood, and their celebrations then adopted several external characteristics from the US holiday, such as giving carnations and other presents to one's mother.
</p><p>The extent of the celebrations varies greatly. In some countries, it is potentially offensive to one's mother not to mark Mother's Day. In others, it is a little-known festival celebrated mainly by immigrants, or covered by the media as a taste of foreign culture.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, the holiday is strongly associated with revering the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In some Catholic homes, families have a special shrine devoted to the <a href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Blessed Virgin Mary">Blessed Virgin Mary</a>. In many Eastern Catholic and Eastern Orthodox Churches, a special prayer service is held in honor of the <a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a> Virgin Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> there is no concept of Mother's Day, but the Quran teaches that children should give priority to loving their mother over their father.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> tradition, Mother's Day is called "<a href="/wiki/Matatirtha_Aunsi" title="Matatirtha Aunsi">Mata Tirtha Aunshi</a>" or "Mother Pilgrimage fortnight", and is celebrated in countries with a Hindu population, especially in Nepal, where mothers are honored with special foods. The holiday is observed on the new moon day in the month of <a href="/wiki/Baisakh" class="mw-redirect" title="Baisakh">Baisakh</a>, i.e., April/May. This celebration is based on Hindu religion and it pre-dates the creation of the US-inspired celebration by at least a few centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, the festival of <a href="/wiki/Ghost_Festival" title="Ghost Festival">Ullambana</a> is derived from the story of <a href="/wiki/Ghost_Festival#Maudgaly.C4.81yana_and_his_mother" title="Ghost Festival">Maudgalyayana and his mother</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Teiser114_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teiser114-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="By_country_.28A.E2.80.93G.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country_(A–G)">By country (A–G)</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Albania">Albania</span></h4>
<p>In Albania, as in a number of Balkan and Eastern European countries, Mother's Day is celebrated on 8 March, in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/International_Women%27s_Day" title="International Women&#39;s Day">International Women's Day</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_world">Arab world</span></h4>
<p>Mother's Day in most Arab countries is celebrated on 21 March. It was introduced in Egypt by journalist <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Amin" title="Mustafa Amin">Mustafa Amin</a><sup id="cite_ref-jehl_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jehl-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and was first celebrated in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The practice has since been copied by other Arab countries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Argentina">Argentina</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on the third Sunday of October. The holiday was originally celebrated on 11 October, the old liturgical date for the celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Solemnity_of_Mary,_Mother_of_God" title="Solemnity of Mary, Mother of God">Maternity of the Blessed Virgin Mary</a> but after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a>, which moved the Virgin Mary festivity to 1 January, the Mother's Day started to be celebrated the third Sunday of October because of popular tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-argentina_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-argentina-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> is the only country in the world that celebrates Mother's Day on this date.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Armenia">Armenia</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on 8 March, and on 7 April as <a href="/wiki/Motherhood_and_Beauty_Day" title="Motherhood and Beauty Day">Maternity and Beauty Day</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span></h4>
<p>In Australia, Mother's Day is celebrated on the second Sunday in May.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Belarus">Belarus</span></h4>
<p>Belarus celebrates Mother's Day on 14 October. Like other ex-Communist republics, Belarus used to celebrate only International Women's Day on 8 March. Mother's Day in Belarus was officially established by the Belarusian government, and it was celebrated for the first time in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-belarusgov_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belarusgov-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The celebration of the Virgin Mary (the holiday of Protection of the Holy Mother of God) is celebrated in the same day.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bhutan">Bhutan</span></h4>
<p>Mother's Day in <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a> is celebrated on 8 May. It was introduced in Bhutan by the Tourism Council of Bhutan.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, Mother's Day (<i>Moederdag</i> or <i>Moederkesdag</i> in <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> and <i>Fête des Mères</i> in <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>) is celebrated on the second Sunday of May. In the week before this holiday children make little presents at primary school, which they give to their mothers in the early morning of Mother's Day. Typically, the father will buy <a href="/wiki/Croissant" title="Croissant">croissants</a> and other sweet breads and pastries and bring these to the mother while she is still in bed – the beginning of a day of pampering for the mother. There are also many people who celebrate Mother's Day on 15 August instead; these are mostly people around <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a>, who consider that day (<a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Mary" title="Assumption of Mary">Assumption</a>) the classical Mother's Day and the observance in May an invention for commercial reasons. It was originally established on that day as the result of a campaign by <a href="/wiki/Frans_Van_Kuyck" title="Frans Van Kuyck">Frans Van Kuyck</a>, a painter and Alderman from Antwerp.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bolivia">Bolivia</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on 27 May. El <i>Día de la Madre Boliviana</i> was passed into law on 8 November 1927, during the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Hernando_Siles_Reyes" title="Hernando Siles Reyes">Hernando Siles Reyes</a>. The date commemorates the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_La_Coronilla&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Battle of La Coronilla (page does not exist)">Battle of La Coronilla</a>, which took place on 27 May 1812, during the <a href="/wiki/Bolivian_War_of_Independence" title="Bolivian War of Independence">Bolivian War of Independence</a>, in what is now the city of <a href="/wiki/Cochabamba" title="Cochabamba">Cochabamba</a>. In this battle, women fighting for the country's independence were slaughtered by the Spanish army. It is not a public holiday, but all schools hold activities and festivities throughout the day.<sup id="cite_ref-bolivia_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bolivia-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazil">Brazil</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on the second Sunday of May. The first Mother's Day in Brazil was promoted by Associação Cristã de Moços de <a href="/wiki/Porto_Alegre" title="Porto Alegre">Porto Alegre</a> (<a href="/wiki/Young_Men%27s_Christian_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="Young Men&#39;s Christian Association">Young Men's Christian Association</a> of Porto Alegre) on 12 May 1918. In 1932, then President <a href="/wiki/Get%C3%BAlio_Vargas" title="Getúlio Vargas">Getúlio Vargas</a> made the second Sunday of May the official date for Mother's Day. In 1947, Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Jaime_de_Barros_C%C3%A2mara" title="Jaime de Barros Câmara">Jaime de Barros Câmara</a>, Cardinal-Archbishop of Rio de Janeiro, decided that this holiday would also be included in the official calendar of the Catholic Church.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Mother's Day is not an official holiday (see <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Brazil" title="Public holidays in Brazil">Public holidays in Brazil</a>), but it is widely observed and typically involves spending time with and giving gifts to one's mother. Because of this, it is considered one of the celebrations most related to <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a> in the country, second only to <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Christmas Day">Christmas Day</a> as the most commercially lucrative holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-brazil_shopping_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brazil_shopping-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span></h4>
<dl><dd><i>See also <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Canada#Other_observances" title="Public holidays in Canada">Other observances</a> in Canada</i></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mother%27s_Day_cake.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Mother%27s_Day_cake.jpg/220px-Mother%27s_Day_cake.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Mother%27s_Day_cake.jpg/330px-Mother%27s_Day_cake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Mother%27s_Day_cake.jpg/440px-Mother%27s_Day_cake.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1127" data-file-height="1127" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mother%27s_Day_cake.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mother's Day cookie cake</div></div></div>
<p>Mother's Day in Canada is celebrated on the second Sunday in May (it is not a public holiday or bank holiday), and typically involves small celebrations and gift-giving to one's mother, grandmother, or other important female figures in one's family.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Celebratory practices are very similar to those of other western nations. A Québécois tradition is for Québécois men to offer roses or other flowers to the women.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span></h4>
<p>Mother's Day is becoming more popular in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. Carnations are a very popular Mother's Day gift and the most sold flowers in relation to the day.<sup id="cite_ref-china_popular_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-china_popular-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In 1997 Mother's Day was set as the day to help poor mothers and to remind people of the poor mothers in rural areas such as China's western region.<sup id="cite_ref-china_popular_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-china_popular-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Daily" title="People&#39;s Daily">People's Daily</a>, the Chinese government's official newspaper, an article explained that "despite originating in the United States, people in China accept the holiday without hesitation because it is in line with the country's traditional ethics – respect for the elderly and filial piety towards parents."<sup id="cite_ref-china_popular_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-china_popular-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In recent years, the Communist Party member Li Hanqiu began to advocate for the official adoption of Mother's Day in memory of Meng Mu, the mother of <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mèng Zǐ</a>. He formed a non-governmental organization called <i>Chinese Mothers' Festival Promotion Society</i>, with the support of 100 Confucian scholars and lecturers of ethics.<sup id="cite_ref-allchina_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allchina-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-china_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-china-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Li and the Society want to replace the Western-style gift of carnations with <a href="/wiki/Lilium" title="Lilium">lilies</a>, which, in ancient times, were planted by Chinese mothers when children left home.<sup id="cite_ref-china_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-china-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Mother's Day remains an unofficial festival, except in a small number of cities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Czech_Republic">Czech Republic</span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated every second Sunday in May. It started in former Czechoslovakia in 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-eastern_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eastern-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The promoter of this celebration was <a href="/wiki/Alice_Masarykov%C3%A1" title="Alice Masaryková">Alice Masaryková</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-eastern_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eastern-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> After World War II communists replaced Mother's Day with International Woman's Day, celebrated on 8 March.<sup id="cite_ref-eastern_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eastern-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The former Czechoslovakia celebrated Women's Day until the <a href="/wiki/Velvet_Revolution" title="Velvet Revolution">Velvet Revolution</a> in 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-eastern_36-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eastern-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> After the split of the country in 1993, the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> started celebrating Mother's Day again.<sup id="cite_ref-eastern_36-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eastern-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt">Egypt</span></h4>
<p>Mother's Day in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> is celebrated on 21 March, the first day of spring in the northern hemisphere. It was introduced in Egypt by journalist <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Amin" title="Mustafa Amin">Mustafa Amin</a><sup id="cite_ref-jehl_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jehl-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> in his book <i>Smiling America</i> (1943). The idea was overlooked at the time. Later Amin heard the story of a widowed mother who devoted her whole life to raising her son until he became a doctor. The son then married and left without showing any gratitude to his mother. Hearing this, Amin became motivated to promote "Mother's Day". The idea was first ridiculed by president <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a> but he eventually accepted it and Mother's Day was first celebrated on 21 March 1956. The practice has since been copied by other Arab countries.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When Mustafa Amin was arrested and imprisoned, there were attempts to change the name of the holiday from "Mother's Day" to "Family Day" as the government wished to prevent the occasion from reminding people of its founder. These attempts were unsuccessful and celebrations continued to be held on that day; classic songs celebrating mothers remain famous to this day.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</span></h4>
<p>Mother's Day is celebrated for three days in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, after the end of rainy season. It comes in mid-fall where people enjoy a three-day feast called "Antrosht".<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the feast, ingredients will be brought by the children for a traditional hash recipe. The ingredients are divided along genders, with girls bringing spices, vegetables, cheese and butter, while the boys bring a lamb or bull. The mother hands out to the family the hash.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A celebration takes place after the meal. The mothers and daughters anoint themselves using butter on their faces and chests. While honoring their family and heroes, men sing songs.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Estonia">Estonia</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, Mother's Day (<i>emadepäev</i> in <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a>) is celebrated on the second Sunday of May. It is recognized nationally, but is not a public holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, Mother's Day (<i>äitienpäivä</i> in <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>) is celebrated on the second Sunday of May. It is recognized nationally, and is  a public holiday. It is usually celebrated at homes where children or grandchildren bring Mother´s day cards that they have drawn to their mothers and grandmothers. Usually some food, coffee and cakes are served for guests. Grown up children visit their parents homes and bring traditionally Mother´s day roses or other flowers accompanied with a Mother´s day card.
The president of Finland honors with medals every year some mothers who have done something exceptional and positive during the year.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, amidst alarm at the low birth rate, there were attempts in 1896 and 1904 to create a national celebration honoring the mothers of large families.<sup id="cite_ref-UFE_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UFE-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In 1906 ten mothers who had nine children each were given an award recognising "High Maternal Merit" ("Haut mérite maternel").<sup id="cite_ref-Artas_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Artas-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> American World War I soldiers fighting in France popularized the US Mother's Day holiday created by Anna Jarvis. They sent so much mail back to their country for Mother's Day that the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Union_Franco-Am%C3%A9ricaine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Union Franco-Américaine (page does not exist)">Union Franco-Américaine</a> created a postal card for that purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-UFE_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UFE-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In 1918, also inspired by Jarvis, the town of Lyon wanted to celebrate a "journée des Mères", but instead decided to celebrate a "Journée Nationale des Mères de familles nombreuses." The holiday was more inspired by anti-depopulation efforts than by the US holiday, with medals awarded to the mothers of large families.<sup id="cite_ref-UFE_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UFE-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The French government made the day official in 1920 as a day for mothers of large families.<sup id="cite_ref-capdevila_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capdevila-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Since then the French government awards the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9daille_de_la_Famille_fran%C3%A7aise" title="Médaille de la Famille française">Médaille de la Famille française</a> to mothers of large families.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1941, by initiative of <a href="/wiki/Philippe_P%C3%A9tain" title="Philippe Pétain">Philippe Pétain</a>, the wartime <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy</a> government used the celebration in support of their policy to encourage larger families, but all mothers were now honored, even mothers with smaller families.<sup id="cite_ref-capdevila_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capdevila-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1950, after the war, the celebration was reinstated. The law of 24 May 1950 required (in Article 1) that the Republic pay official homage to French Mothers. Article 2 stated it should be celebrated on the last Sunday in May as the "Fête des Mères" (except when Pentecost fell on that day, in which case it was moved to the first Sunday in June). Article 3 stated that all expenditure shall be covered from the budget of the Ministry of Public Health and Population.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1950s, the celebration lost all its patriotic and natalist ideologies, and became heavily commercialized.<sup id="cite_ref-UFE_74-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UFE-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1956, the celebration was given a budget and integrated into the new <a href="/w/index.php?title=Code_de_l%27action_Sociale_et_des_familles&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Code de l&#39;action Sociale et des familles (page does not exist)">Code de l'action Sociale et des familles</a>. In 2004 responsibility for the holiday was transferred to the Minister responsible for families.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Georgia">Georgia</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> celebrates Mother's Day on 3 March. It was declared by the first President of Georgia <a href="/wiki/Zviad_Gamsakhurdia" title="Zviad Gamsakhurdia">Zviad Gamsakhurdia</a> in order to replace the International Women Day, and it was officially approved by the Supreme Council in 1991. Nowadays Georgia celebrates both Mother's Day on 3 March and International Women's Day on 8 March.<sup id="cite_ref-georgia_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-georgia-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herztorte_zum_Muttertag.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Herztorte_zum_Muttertag.jpg/220px-Herztorte_zum_Muttertag.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Herztorte_zum_Muttertag.jpg/330px-Herztorte_zum_Muttertag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Herztorte_zum_Muttertag.jpg/440px-Herztorte_zum_Muttertag.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herztorte_zum_Muttertag.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mother's Day cake in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the 1920s, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> had the lowest birthrate in Europe, and the declining trend was continuing. This was attributed to women's participation in the labor market. At the same time, influential groups in society (politicians of left and right, churchwomen, and feminists) believed that mothers should be honored but could not agree on how to do so. However, all groups strongly agreed on the promotion of the values of motherhood. In 1923, this resulted in the unanimous adoption of <i>Muttertag</i>, the Mother's Day holiday as imported from America<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and Norway. The head of the Association of German Florists cited "the inner conflict of our <i><a href="/wiki/Volk" title="Volk">Volk</a></i> and the loosening of the family" as his reason for introducing the holiday. He expected that the holiday would unite the divided country. In 1925, the Mother's Day Committee joined the task force for the recovery of the <i>volk</i>, and the holiday stopped depending on commercial interests and began emphasizing the need to increase the population in Germany by promoting motherhood.<sup id="cite_ref-mouton_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mouton-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The holiday was then seen as a means to encourage women to bear more children, which nationalists saw as a way to rejuvenate the nation. The holiday did not celebrate individual women, but an idealized standard of motherhood. The progressive forces resisted the implementation of the holiday because it was backed by so many conservatives, and because they saw it as a way to eliminate the rights of working women. <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Die_Frau&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Die Frau (page does not exist)">Die Frau</a></i>, the newspaper of the Federation of German Women's Associations, refused to recognize the holiday. Many local authorities adopted their own interpretation of the holiday: it would be a day to support economically larger families or single-mother families. The guidelines for the subsidies had <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a> criteria, but there is no indication that social workers ever implemented them in practice, and subsidies were given preferentially to families in economic need rather than to families with more children or "healthier" children.<sup id="cite_ref-mouton_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mouton-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the Nazi party in power during 1933–1945, the situation changed radically. The promotion of Mother's Day increased in many European countries, including the UK and France. From the position of the German Nazi government, the role of mothers was to give healthy children to the German nation. The Nazi party's intention was to create a pure "Aryan race" according to <a href="/wiki/Nazi_eugenics" title="Nazi eugenics">nazi eugenics</a>. Among other Mother's Day ideas, the government promoted the death of a mother's sons in battle as the highest embodiment of patriotic motherhood.<sup id="cite_ref-mouton_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mouton-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taylor_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Nazis quickly declared Mother's Day an official holiday and put it under the control of the NSV (<a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Socialist_People%27s_Welfare_Association&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="National Socialist People&#39;s Welfare Association (page does not exist)">National Socialist People's Welfare Association</a>) and the NSF (<a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Socialist_Women_Organization&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="National Socialist Women Organization (page does not exist)">National Socialist Women Organization</a>). This created conflicts with other organizations that resented Nazi control of the holiday, including Catholic and Protestant churches and local women's organizations. Local authorities resisted the guidelines from the Nazi government and continued assigning resources to families who were in economic need, much to the dismay of the Nazi officials.<sup id="cite_ref-mouton_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mouton-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muttertagsfeier_im_Unrralager.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Muttertagsfeier_im_Unrralager.jpg/220px-Muttertagsfeier_im_Unrralager.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Muttertagsfeier_im_Unrralager.jpg/330px-Muttertagsfeier_im_Unrralager.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Muttertagsfeier_im_Unrralager.jpg/440px-Muttertagsfeier_im_Unrralager.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="524" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muttertagsfeier_im_Unrralager.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mother's Day in <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Relief_and_Rehabilitation_Administration" title="United Nations Relief and Rehabilitation Administration">UNRRA</a> camp Germany in 1946</div></div></div>
<p>In 1938, the government began issuing an award called <a href="/wiki/Cross_of_Honor_of_the_German_Mother" class="mw-redirect" title="Cross of Honor of the German Mother">Mother's Cross</a> (<i>Mutterkreuz</i>), according to categories that depended on the number of children a mother had. The medal was awarded on Mother's Day and also on other holidays due to the large number of recipients. The Cross was an effort to encourage women to have more children, and recipients were required to have at least four.<sup id="cite_ref-mouton_80-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mouton-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taylor_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="By_country_.28H.E2.80.93M.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country_(H–M)">By country (H–M)</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hungary">Hungary</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on the first Sunday of May. It was first celebrated in 1925<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> by the Hungarian <a href="/wiki/Red_Cross_Youth" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Cross Youth">Red Cross Youth</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span></h4>
<p>The modern Mother's Day has been assimilated into <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> culture<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> and is celebrated every year on the second Sunday of May.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Indians do not celebrate the occasion as a religious event; its celebration is mostly restricted to urban areas where the occasion has been largely commercialized.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">Indonesia</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesian</a> Mother's Day (<a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Indonesian</a>: <i lang="id"><b>Hari Ibu</b></i>) is celebrated nationally on 22 December. The date was made an official holiday by President <a href="/wiki/Sukarno" title="Sukarno">Sukarno</a> under Presidential Decree No. 316/1953, on the 25th anniversary of the 1928 Indonesian Women Congress. The day originally sought to celebrate the spirit of Indonesian women and to improve the condition of the nation. Today, the meaning of Mother's Day has changed, and it is celebrated by expressing love and gratitude to mothers. People present gifts to mothers (such as flowers) and hold surprise parties and competitions, which include cooking and <a href="/wiki/Kebaya" title="Kebaya">kebaya</a> wearing. People also allow mothers a day off from domestic chores.<sup id="cite_ref-wardhani_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wardhani-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The holiday is celebrated on the anniversary of the opening day of the first Indonesian Women Congress (<a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Indonesian</a>: <i lang="id">Kongres Perempuan Indonesia</i>), which was held from 22 to 25 December 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-indonesia_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indonesia-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bulbeck_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bulbeck-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The Congress took place in a building called Dalem Jayadipuran, which now serves as the office of the Center of History and Traditional Values Preservation (<a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Indonesian</a>: <i lang="id">Balai Pelestarian Sejarah dan Nilai Tradisional</i>) in Brigjen Katamso Street, <a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta" title="Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a>. The Congress was attended by 30 feminist organizations from 12 cities in <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>. In Indonesia, feminist organizations have existed since 1912, inspired by Indonesian heroines of the 19th century, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Kartini" title="Kartini">Kartini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martha_Christina_Tiahahu" title="Martha Christina Tiahahu">Martha Christina Tiahahu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cut_Nyak_Meutia" title="Cut Nyak Meutia">Cut Nyak Meutia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maria_Walanda_Maramis" title="Maria Walanda Maramis">Maria Walanda Maramis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dewi_Sartika" title="Dewi Sartika">Dewi Sartika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyai_Ahmad_Dahlan" title="Nyai Ahmad Dahlan">Nyai Ahmad Dahlan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rasuna_Said" title="Rasuna Said">Rasuna Said</a>, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-indonesia_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indonesia-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The Congress intended to improve women's rights in education and marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-robinson_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-robinson-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Indonesia also celebrates the Kartini Day (<a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Indonesian</a>: <i lang="id"><b>Hari Kartini</b></i>) on 21 April, in memory of activist <a href="/wiki/Raden_Ajeng_Kartini" class="mw-redirect" title="Raden Ajeng Kartini">Raden Ajeng Kartini</a>. This is a celebration of the emancipation of women.<sup id="cite_ref-bulbeck_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bulbeck-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The observance was instituted at the 1938 Indonesian Women Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-robinson_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-robinson-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During President <a href="/wiki/Suharto" title="Suharto">Suharto</a>'s <a href="/wiki/New_Order_(Indonesia)" title="New Order (Indonesia)">New Order</a> (1965–1998), government propaganda used Mother's Day and Kartini Day to inculcate into women the idea that they should be docile and stay at home.<sup id="cite_ref-robinson_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-robinson-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Commemorative_gold_medal_issued_in_the_Pahlavi_era_on_the_occasion_of_Mother%27s_Day.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Commemorative_gold_medal_issued_in_the_Pahlavi_era_on_the_occasion_of_Mother%27s_Day.jpg/220px-Commemorative_gold_medal_issued_in_the_Pahlavi_era_on_the_occasion_of_Mother%27s_Day.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Commemorative_gold_medal_issued_in_the_Pahlavi_era_on_the_occasion_of_Mother%27s_Day.jpg/330px-Commemorative_gold_medal_issued_in_the_Pahlavi_era_on_the_occasion_of_Mother%27s_Day.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Commemorative_gold_medal_issued_in_the_Pahlavi_era_on_the_occasion_of_Mother%27s_Day.jpg/440px-Commemorative_gold_medal_issued_in_the_Pahlavi_era_on_the_occasion_of_Mother%27s_Day.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="395" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Commemorative_gold_medal_issued_in_the_Pahlavi_era_on_the_occasion_of_Mother%27s_Day.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Commemorative gold medal issued in the <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_dynasty" title="Pahlavi dynasty">Pahlavi</a> era on the occasion of Mother's Day, dated 1975. <b>Obv</b>: Bust of Queen <a href="/wiki/Farah_Pahlavi" title="Farah Pahlavi">Farah Pahlavi</a>. <b>Rev</b>:  Mother and children standing around a seated Farah Pahlavi, holding open book</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on 20 <a href="/wiki/Jumada_al-thani" class="mw-redirect" title="Jumada al-thani">Jumada al-thani</a>. This is the sixth month in the Islamic calendar (a lunar calendar) and every year the holiday falls on a different day of the Gregorian calendar. This is the birthday anniversary of <a href="/wiki/Fatimah" title="Fatimah">Fatimah</a>, Prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>'s only daughter according to <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-debano_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debano-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iranpresident_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iranpresident-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> On this day, banners reading "<i>Ya Fatemeah</i> (O! Fatemeh)" are displayed on "government buildings, private buildings, public streets and car windows."<sup id="cite_ref-debano_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debano-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Mother's Day was originally observed on 16 December but the date was changed after the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">Iranian Revolution</a> in 1979. The celebration is both Women's Day (replacing International Women's Day) and Mother's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-debano_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debano-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-haeri1_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haeri1-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1960, the Institute for Women Protection adopted the Western holiday and established it on 25 <a href="/wiki/Solar_Hijri_calendar" title="Solar Hijri calendar">Azar</a> (16 December), the date the Institute was founded. The Institute's action had the support of Queen <a href="/wiki/Farah_Pahlavi" title="Farah Pahlavi">Farah Pahlavi</a>, the wife of the last Shah of Persia, who promoted the construction of maternity clinics in remote parts of the country to commemorate the day.<sup id="cite_ref-Sabet_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sabet-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_regime" class="mw-redirect" title="Pahlavi regime">Pahlavi regime</a> used the holiday to promote "gender ideologies" of the regime.<sup id="cite_ref-debano_50-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debano-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The Shah's government honored and gave awards to women who represented the idealized view of the regime, including mothers who had many healthy children.<sup id="cite_ref-Sabet_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sabet-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Shahla Haeri, the Islamic Republic government has used the holiday to "control and channel women's movements" and to promote role models for the traditional concept of family.<sup id="cite_ref-haeri2_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haeri2-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Fatimah is seen by these critics as the chosen model of a woman completely dedicated to certain traditionally sanctioned feminine roles.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> However, supporters of the choice contend that there is much more to her life story than simply such "traditional" roles.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Israel">Israel</span></h4>
<p>The Jewish population of Israel used to celebrate Mother's Day on <a href="/wiki/Shevat" title="Shevat">Shevat</a> 30 of the Jewish calendar, which falls between 30 January and 1 March. The celebration was set as the same date that <a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Szold" title="Henrietta Szold">Henrietta Szold</a> died (13 February 1945). Henrietta had no biological children, but her organization <a href="/wiki/Youth_Aliyah" title="Youth Aliyah">Youth Aliyah</a> rescued many Jewish children from Nazi Germany and provided for them. She also championed children's rights. Szold is considered the "mother" of all those children, and that is why her annual remembrance day (יום השנה) was set as Mother's Day (יוֹם הָאֵם, yom ha'em). The holiday has evolved over time, becoming a celebration of mutual love inside the family, called Family Day (יוֹם הַמִשְּפָּחָה, yom hamishpacha). This holiday is mainly celebrated in preschools with an activity to which parents are invited.  Mother's Day is mainly celebrated by children at kindergartens. There are no longer mutual gifts among members of the family, and there is no longer any commercialization of the celebration. It is not an official holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-israel_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-israel-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span></h4>
<p>Mother's Day in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> was celebrated for the first time on 24 December 1933 as the "Day of the mother and the child" (<i>Giornata della madre e del fanciullo</i>). It was instituted by the <a href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/ONMI" class="extiw" title="it:ONMI">Opera nazionale maternità e infanzia</a> in order to publicly reward the most prolific Italian women every year.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Mother's Day was first celebrated on 12 May 1957 in <a href="/wiki/Assisi" title="Assisi">Assisi</a>, at the initiative of Reverend Otello Migliosi, the <a href="/wiki/Parish_priest" class="mw-redirect" title="Parish priest">parish priest</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tordibetto" title="Tordibetto">Tordibetto</a> church.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> This celebration was so popular that in the following year Mother's Day was adopted throughout Italy. On 18 December 1958, a proposal was presented to the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_the_Republic_(Italy)" title="Senate of the Republic (Italy)">Italian Senate</a> to make the holiday official.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, Mother's Day<span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">母の日</span><span style="display:none">,</span>&#32;<i>Haha no Hi</i>)</span> was initially commemorated during the <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Shōwa period">Shōwa period</a> as the birthday of <a href="/wiki/Empress_K%C5%8Djun" title="Empress Kōjun">Empress Kōjun</a> (mother of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Akihito" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Akihito">Emperor Akihito</a>) on 6 March. This was established in 1931 when the Imperial Women's Union was organized. In 1937, the first meeting of "Praise Mothers" was held on 8 May, and in 1949 Japanese society adopted the second Sunday of May as the official date for Mother's Day in Japan. Today, people typically give their mothers gifts of flowers such as red <a href="/wiki/Carnation" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnation">carnations</a><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">roses</a>.Japan is most known for giving carnations on Mother's Day.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on 19 May every year. The holiday was first celebrated in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>  Mothers are also honored on <a href="/wiki/International_Women%27s_Day" title="International Women&#39;s Day">International Women's Day</a><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about a holiday celebrating mothers and motherhood. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Mother%27s_Day_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Mother&#39;s Day (disambiguation)">Mother's Day (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latvia">Latvia</span></h4>
<p>Mother's Day in <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> was celebrated for the first time in 1922. Since 1934, Mother's Day is celebrated on the second Sunday of May.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> After the end of the soviet <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Baltic_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Occupation of Baltic states">occupation of Baltic states</a> celebration was resumed in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Mothers are also honored on <a href="/wiki/International_Women%27s_Day" title="International Women&#39;s Day">International Women's Day</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lithuania">Lithuania</span></h4>
<p>Mother's Day in Lithuania was celebrated for the first time in 1928. In Lithuania, Mother's Day is celebrated on the first Sunday of May.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malawi">Malawi</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>. Mother's Day is a public holiday. The day is observed on 15 October or the following workday. It is celebrated on the UN's World Rural Women's Day.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Maldives">Maldives</span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on 13 May. The day is celebrated in different ways. Children give gifts and spend time with their mothers. Daughters give their mothers cards and handmade gifts and sons give their mothers gifts and flowers. Maldivians love to celebrate Mother's day, and they have it specially written on their calendar.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malta">Malta</span></h4>
<p>The first mention of Mother's Day in <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> occurred during the Radio Children's Programmes run by Frans H. Said in May 1961. Within a few years, Mother's Day became one of the most popular dates in the Maltese calendar. In Malta, this day is commemorated on the second Sunday in May. Mothers are invariably given gifts and invited for lunch, usually at a good quality restaurant.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">Mexico</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Mexico#Festivities" title="Public holidays in Mexico">Public holidays in Mexico §&#160;Festivities</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, the government of <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_Obreg%C3%B3n" title="Álvaro Obregón">Álvaro Obregón</a> imported the Mother's Day holiday from the US in 1922, and the newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Exc%C3%A9lsior" title="Excélsior">Excélsior</a></i> held a massive promotional campaign for the holiday that year.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_133_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_133-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The conservative government tried to use the holiday to promote a more conservative role for mothers in families, but that perspective was criticized by the socialists as promoting an unrealistic image of a woman who was not good for much more than breeding.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_133_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_133-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the mid-1930s, the leftist government of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A1zaro_C%C3%A1rdenas" title="Lázaro Cárdenas">Lázaro Cárdenas</a> promoted the holiday as a "patriotic festival". The Cárdenas government tried to use the holiday as a vehicle for various efforts: to stress the importance of families as the basis for national development; to benefit from the loyalty that Mexicans felt towards their mothers; to introduce new morals to Mexican women; and to reduce the influence that the church and the Catholic right exerted over women.<sup id="cite_ref-sherman_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sherman-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The government sponsored the holiday in the schools.<sup id="cite_ref-sherman_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sherman-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> However, ignoring the strict guidelines from the government, theatre plays were filled with religious icons and themes. Consequently, the "national celebrations" became "religious fiestas" despite the efforts of the government.<sup id="cite_ref-sherman_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sherman-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Soledad Orozco García, the wife of President <a href="/wiki/Manuel_%C3%81vila_Camacho" title="Manuel Ávila Camacho">Manuel Ávila Camacho</a>, promoted the holiday during the 1940s, resulting in an important state-sponsored celebration.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_133–134_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_133–134-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The 1942 celebration lasted a full week and included an announcement that all women could reclaim their pawned sewing machines from the <a href="/wiki/Nacional_Monte_de_Piedad" title="Nacional Monte de Piedad">Monte de Piedad</a> at no cost.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_133–134_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_133–134-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to Orozco's promotion, the Catholic <a href="/wiki/National_Synarchist_Union" title="National Synarchist Union">National Synarchist Union</a> (UNS) took heed of the holiday around 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_134_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_134-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Shop-owner members of the Party of the Mexican Revolution (now the <a href="/wiki/Institutional_Revolutionary_Party" title="Institutional Revolutionary Party">Institutional Revolutionary Party</a>) observed a custom allowing women from humble classes to pick a free Mother's Day gift from a shop to bring home to their families. The Synarchists worried that this promoted both materialism and the idleness of lower classes, and in turn, reinforced the systemic social problems of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_134–135_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_134–135-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Currently this holiday practice is viewed as very conservative, but the 1940s' UNS saw Mother's Day as part of the larger debate on the modernization that was happening at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_135–136_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_135–136-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> This economic modernization was inspired by US models and was sponsored by the state. The fact that the holiday was originally imported from the US was seen as evidence of an attempt at imposing capitalism and materialism in Mexican society.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_135–136_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_135–136-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The UNS and the clergy of the city of <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n,_Guanajuato" title="León, Guanajuato">León</a> interpreted the government's actions as an effort to secularize the holiday and to promote a more active role for women in society. They concluded that the government's long-term goal was to cause women to abandon their traditional roles at home in order to spiritually weaken men.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_135–136_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_135–136-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> They also saw the holiday as an attempt to secularize the cult to the Virgin Mary, inside a larger effort to dechristianize several holidays. The government sought to counter these claims by organizing widespread masses and asking religious women to assist with the state-sponsored events in order to "depaganize" them.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_136–139_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_136–139-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The clergy preferred to promote 2 July celebration of the Santísima <a href="/w/index.php?title=Virgen_de_la_Luz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Virgen de la Luz (page does not exist)">Virgen de la Luz</a>, the patron of <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n,_Guanajuato" title="León, Guanajuato">León, Guanajuato</a>, in replacement of Mother's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_134_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_134-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In 1942, at the same time as Soledad's greatest celebration of Mother's Day, the clergy organized the 210th celebration of the Virgin Mary with a large parade in León.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_136–139_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_136–139-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a consensus among scholars that the Mexican government abandoned its revolutionary initiatives during the 1940s, including its efforts to influence Mother's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-newcomer_134_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newcomer_134-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Today the "Día de las Madres" is an unofficial holiday in Mexico held each year on 10 May,<sup id="cite_ref-legacy_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legacy-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> the day on which it was first celebrated in Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Mexico, to show affection and appreciation to the mother, it is traditional to start the celebration with the famous song "Las Mañanitas", either a cappella, with the help of a mariachi or a contracted trio. Families usually gather to celebrate, trying to spend as much time as possible with mothers to honor them. They bring some dishes and eat together or visit a restaurant.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="By_country_.28N.E2.80.93S.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country_(N–S)">By country (N–S)</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nepal">Nepal</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, there is a festival equivalent to Mother's Day, called Mata Tirtha Aunsi ("Mother Pilgrimage New Moon"), or Mata Tirtha <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Buddhism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Puja (Buddhism)">Puja</a> ("Mother Pilgrimage Worship"). It is celebrated according to the lunar calendar. It falls on the last day of the dark fortnight in the month of <a href="/wiki/Baishakh_(Nepali_calendar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Baishakh (Nepali calendar)">Baishakh</a> which falls in April–May (in 2015, it will occur on 18 April). The dark fortnight lasts for 15 days from the full moon to the new moon. This festival is observed to commemorate and honor mothers, and it is celebrated by giving gifts to mothers and remembering mothers who are no more.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>To honor mothers who have died, it is the tradition to go on a pilgrimage to the Mata Tirtha ponds, located 6&#160;km to the southwest of downtown <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu" title="Kathmandu">Kathmandu</a>. The nearby <a href="/wiki/Mata_Tirtha" class="mw-redirect" title="Mata Tirtha">Mata Tirtha</a> village is named after these ponds. Previously, the tradition was observed primarily by the <a href="/wiki/Newar" class="mw-redirect" title="Newar">Newar</a> community and other people living in the <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu_Valley" title="Kathmandu Valley">Kathmandu Valley</a>. Now this festival is widely celebrated across the country.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Many tragic <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a> legends have been created, suggesting different reasons why this pond became a pilgrimage site. The most popular version says that, in ancient times, the mother of a shepherd died, and he made offerings to a nearby pond. There he saw the face of his mother in the water, with her hand taking the offerings. Since then, many people visited the pond, hoping to see their deceased mother's face. Pilgrims believe that they will bring peace to their mothers' souls by visiting the sacred place. There are two ponds. The larger one is for ritual bathing. The smaller one is used to "look upon mother's face", and is fenced by iron bars to prevent people from bathing in it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, in the Kathmandu valley the South-Western corner is reserved for women and women-related rituals, and the North-Eastern is for men and men-related rituals. The worship place for Mata Tirtha Aunsi is located in Mata Tirtha in the South-Western half of the valley, while the worship place for <a href="/wiki/Gokarna_Aunsi" title="Gokarna Aunsi">Gokarna Aunsi</a>, the equivalent celebration for deceased fathers, is located in <a href="/wiki/Gokarna,_Nepal" class="mw-redirect" title="Gokarna, Nepal">Gokarna, Nepal</a>, in the North-Eastern half. This division is reflected in many aspects of the life in Kathmandu valley.<sup id="cite_ref-HeestermanHoek1992_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HeestermanHoek1992-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mother's Day is known as Aama ko Mukh Herne Din in <a href="/wiki/Nepali_language" title="Nepali language">Nepali</a>, which literally means "day to see mother's face". In <a href="/wiki/Nepal_Bhasa" class="mw-redirect" title="Nepal Bhasa">Nepal Bhasa</a>, the festival is known as Mām yā Khwā Swayegu, which can be translated as "to look upon mother's face".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, Mother's Day was introduced as early as 1910 by the Dutch branch of the <a href="/wiki/Salvation_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Salvation Army">Salvation Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The Royal Dutch Society for Horticulture and Botany, a group protecting the interest of Dutch florists, worked to promote the holiday; they hoped to emulate the commercial success achieved by American florists.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_208_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_208-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> They were imitating the campaign already underway by florists in Germany and Austria, but they were aware that the traditions had originated in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_208_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_208-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Florists launched a major promotional effort in 1925.
